SECTION 1.  
PROJECT CONSIDERATIONS 
The proposed project site is located off 3rd Street to the northwest of the existing 
Dry Dock 3 of the Pearl Harbor Naval Shipyard and Intermediate Maintenance Facility (PHNSY & 
IMF) in Pearl Harbor on the Island of Oahu, Hawaii. The project location and general vicinity are 
shown on the Project Location Map, Plate 1. 
The proposed new dry dock facility will be located in the Controlled Industrial Area (CIA) 
between Dry Dock 3 and the abandoned Marine Railway No. 2.  It is envisioned that the dry dock 
will be a concrete gravity structure approximately 650 feet long and 100 feet wide with a depth of 
about 55 feet.  Fill will be required to establish grade given that much of the area surrounding the 
dry dock is currently in water.  The areas surrounding the dry dock will be paved to support vehicular 
traffic and production shop spaces. 
A design-level field exploration and laboratory testing program consisting of both land 
and water borings was undertaken to assist the design team in the preliminary design of the 
project. This geotechnical data report specifically addresses the field exploration and laboratory 
testing program for the water boring work only.  A geotechnical data report for the land boring 
work has been transmitted under separate cover. 
A summary of the field exploration program for the water borings conducted for the 
project is presented below. Detailed information and discussions for each specific work item are 
provided in the respective appendices of this geotechnical data report. The as-drilled 
investigation locations are shown on the Boring Location Plan, Plate 2.

SECTION 2.  
SCOPE OF WORK 
The purpose of our field exploration and laboratory testing program is to obtain an 
overview of the subsurface conditions at the Dry Dock 3 Replacement site in support of the design 
of the new dry dock facility project. 
Our work was performed in accordance with the scope of services outlined in our fee 
proposal dated April 24, 2020, and the Technical Services Amendment No. 3 effective 
June 15, 2020. In order to accomplish this, we conducted a field exploration and laboratory 
testing program consisting of the following tasks and efforts: 
1. 
Research and review of in-house geologic and soil information in the project 
vicinity. A list of the references used in the preparation of this geotechnical data 
report is presented in the reference section. 
2. 
Procurement of base passes for our field personnel and subcontractors for access 
to the project site, including DBIDS for JBPHH and CIA badges for PHNSY & IMF. 
3. 
Preparation and submittal of the following project documents in support of our 
field exploration activities and briefing of our field personnel of the plans: 
Geotechnical Investigation Work Plan – Geolabs, Inc. (July 2020) 
Accident Prevention Plan with Activity Hazard Analysis – Geolabs, Inc. 
(June 2020) 
4. 
Surveying of the proposed boring locations by ControlPoint Surveying, Inc. 
5. 
Implementation of best management practices (BMPs) during our field 
exploration work, including installation of silt curtain around the landing craft 
during the water boring exploration. 
6. 
Provision of a dedicated Site Safety and Health Officer (SSHO) during our field 
exploration work. 
7. 
Performance of energy measurement of standard penetration test (SPT) 
automatic trip hammer system. 
8. 
Preparation of a dive plan and meeting with Pearl Harbor Designated Dive 
Coordinator (DDC) prior to the field exploration work. 
9. 
Conduct a dive survey, using qualified personnel, at the water boring locations to 
assess the presence of protected natural resources, corals, and seagrasses as

Coordination of the field exploration and logging of the borings by our field 
engineer/geologist.

Provision of 55-gallon drums to contain the drill spoils and water generated from

a designated area of the project site by our office for subsequent testing and 
disposal of the investigation derived wastes (IDW) by our subcontractor, Element

SECTION 3.  
SITE CHARACTERIZATION 
3.1 
Regional Geology 
The Island of Oahu was built by the extrusion of basaltic lavas from two shield volcanoes, 
Waianae and Koolau. The older volcano, Waianae, is estimated to be middle to late Pliocene in 
age and forms the bulk of the western one-third of the island. The younger shield volcano, 
Koolau, is estimated to be late Pliocene to early Pleistocene (Ice Age) in age and forms the 
majority of the eastern two-thirds of the island. The Koolau shield reached the end of its main 
shield-building phase about 2 million years ago. 
The geologic conditions underlying the project site are the result of a complex series of 
deposition and erosional events brought on by glacio-eustatic sea level changes and 
post-erosional volcanic activities during the Pleistocene Epoch. Following the cessation of 
volcanic activity of the Koolau Volcano, alluvial soil materials were deposited in a deltaic or 
estuarine environment in the area of Pearl Harbor. 
The sea level fluctuated several times, receding perhaps 300 feet below and rising about 
100 feet above the present sea level. Concurrent with a rise in sea level during the late 
Pleistocene Epoch, eruption of the Salt Lake-Makalapa member of the Honolulu Volcanic Series 
mantled the older alluvium with thick deposits of volcanic ash, which consolidated to form 
volcanic tuff (locally known as “mudrock”) layers.  Subsequent erosion dissected and thinned 
those mantles of volcanic tuff.  
During the recent epoch, as sea level rose to its current stand, the area was covered with 
soft and loose lagoonal marine deposits. These deposits were subsequently covered with 
man-made fills placed during the middle portion of this century for land reclamation projects 
during construction of the existing Pearl Harbor Naval Shipyard.  The waterfront facility project 
site is located at the edge of the former Pearl Harbor shoreline. The subsurface conditions of the 
project site generally consist of fill materials overlying coralline deposits and highly weathered 
volcanic tuff materials.

3.2 
Subsurface Characteristics 
In general, we observed two main stratigraphic units within the water borings drilled for 
the proposed dry dock waterfront facility. The stratigraphic units consist of soil and rock materials 
of varying character and engineering properties. The two main stratigraphic units are listed below 
in the general order of geologic age, beginning with the younger unit and ending with the older 
unit: 
1. 
Coral Limestone (Qls) 
2. 
Older Alluvium (Qa) 
It should be noted that the harbor sediments are generally underlain by interbedded 
layers of coralline detritus and older alluvium.  The following sections briefly describe the general 
characteristics of each of the above stratigraphic units based on our literature research, 
experience with similar materials, and the field exploration and laboratory testing program 
conducted at selected locations across the project site.  
3.2.1 Coral Limestone (Qls) 
This unit consists of fossil remnants of coral-algal reefs and their related detrital debris 
deposits. In Hawaii, the detrital deposits tend to be the dominant portion of any coralline 
rock assemblage. The detrital deposits are weakly to strongly cemented coralline debris 
consisting of gravel, sand, and silt-sized clastic fragments. Some of the coralline fragments 
were dissolved, and the solution cavities were filled with cemented and/or uncemented 
fine sands and silts. The following subunits may be used in this geotechnical data report. 
Coralline Detritus 
This subunit consists of granular detritus broken from reef formations. The soils 
generally consist of medium dense to dense sandy gravel and gravelly sands. Silts 
and clays are only present in minor or trace amounts. Detritus also may be weakly 
cemented with occasional moderate to strong cementation. 
Coral Formation 
This subunit is composed of coral and calcareous algae in their original growth 
positions or detrital deposits, which have become moderately to well cemented. 
SECTION 3. SITE CHARACTERIZATION  
W.O. 8058-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 7 
Hawaii  California 
Sandstone 
Sandstone is a sedimentary rock composed mainly of cemented sand-sized 
particles (clasts) consisting of individual mineral particles or grains of the 
weathered parent rock. The sedimentary particles comprising sandstone typically 
are derived from the breakdown of parent material into sedimentary particles. 
The sedimentary particles typically are eroded, transported and sorted by water 
or air, and deposited as layers of sedimentary materials. Over time, the sediments 
may be buried by other sediments or geologic layers, and the burial pressure 
combined with a cementing agent act to consolidate the grains and cement the 
pore space between the grains to form hardened sandstone. Typical cementing 
agents may be calcium carbonate (common in calcareous sand deposits of Hawaii) 
or silica and some clays.  
Most sandstones in Hawaii are light brown in color and calcareous being derived 
from the breakdown of parent coralline and reef materials into sand-sized 
particles. The cementing agent in most calcareous sandstone is calcium carbonate. 
Inclusions of basaltic rock fragments, marine shells, and other fragments of broken 
coral may be cemented in the sandstone layers. Sandstones containing such clast 
inclusions commonly are referred to as beach rock in Hawaii, having been derived 
from available shoreline geologic materials.  
The strength (hardness) of sandstone generally depends on the grain size, degree 
of sorting, and degree of cementation of the sedimentary materials. The 
weathering and decomposition of calcareous sandstone may be accelerated by 
exposure to acidic water, which attacks the calcareous cement binding the sand 
grains and causes a slow exfoliation of the outermost exposed layers of the 
sandstone outcropping. Sandstone may or may not contain visible bedding 
surfaces. Aeolian (wind derived) sandstone may exhibit cross-bedding structures 
that developed during the initial deposition of the sand layers. 
SECTION 3. SITE CHARACTERIZATION  
W.O. 8058-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 8 
Hawaii  California 
3.2.2 Older Alluvium (Qa) 
Older alluvial deposits are generally very stiff to hard silty and clayey soils that originally 
were deposited in alluvial environments, buried over long periods of time by more recent 
deposits to become consolidated, and then subsequently uncovered at the ground 
surface by erosion or other geologic processes. This unit generally overlies the Koolau 
Basalt and consists of terrigenous sediments, which have been transported by stream 
action from the upper reaches of the streams.  
Gravel lenses representing buried stream channels are sometimes encountered. Portions 
of the Older Alluvium unit contain some weathered basaltic cobbles and boulders. These 
coarse-grained parts of the unit also may contain artesian groundwater from the basal 
freshwater system or as a sub-aquifer. The deposits may resemble saprolite soil deposits, 
but they are clearly differentiable by the layered characteristics. Frequently, these 
materials show a moderate to high shrink/swell potential when subjected to fluctuations 
in moisture. The following subunits may be used in this geotechnical data report. 
Older Alluvium 
This subunit generally consists of tan to brown silty clays with varying amounts of 
sands and infrequent pockets of gravel and cobbles. The consistency is generally 
in the range of very stiff to hard.  
Old Lagoonal Deposit 
This subunit generally consists of light gray to medium gray sandy silts or silty 
sands in stiff to very stiff consistency and/or medium dense to dense relative 
density with various degrees of cementation. In some cases, strongly cemented 
deposits form siltstones. Gravel-sized fragments are rare. Frequently, the silts are 
organic in origin and may have a mild to strong organic odor.  
We explored the subsurface conditions across the project site by drilling and sampling six 
water borings, designated as Boring Nos. WB-1, SB-2, WB-3, WB-4, WB-5, and WB-6A, extending 
to depths ranging from about 185 to 200.5 feet below the water surface. The approximate boring 
locations are shown on the Boring Location Plan, Plate 2. 
SECTION 3. SITE CHARACTERIZATION

Page 9 
Hawaii  California 
Our field exploration encountered the mudline at depths ranging from about 31 to 51 feet 
below the water surface. Harbor sediments consisting of very soft silty clays and very loose silty 
gravel were encountered to depths ranging between about 35.5 and 59 feet below the water 
surface (or about 4.5 to 9.5 feet below the mudline).  The harbor sediments were underlain by 
interbedded layers of coralline detritus consisting of loose to dense coralline sands and gravel 
and older alluvial soil deposits consisting of stiff to hard silty clays and clayey silts, extending to 
the maximum depth explored of 200.5 feet below the water surface. 
Detailed descriptions of our field exploration methodology and the Logs of Borings are 
presented in Appendix A. Results of the laboratory tests performed on selected soil samples are 
presented in Appendix B. Photographs of the core samples retrieved from our field exploration 
are presented in Appendix C.

SECTION 4.  
GEOTECHNICAL EXPLORATION PROGRAM 
The geotechnical exploration program conducted for the water borings for the Dry Dock 3 
Replacement project generally consisted of the following field and laboratory work components. 
A brief description of each work component is presented below. Detailed discussions, findings, 
and test results of the various components are provided in the respective appendices herein. 
4.1 
Soil Borings, Rock Coring, and Associated Field Testing 
Soil and rock sampling methods and field testing were performed in accordance with the 
Geotechnical Investigation Work Plan dated July 1, 2020, and the Engineering Specification for 
Subsurface Investigation and Laboratory Testing (Rev. 003) dated April 7, 2020 by Bechtel 
National, Inc. The field exploration is described in more detail in the introduction to Appendix A. 
The following discussion provides an overview of the methods and procedures. 
The overwater work was conducted from a twin-screw workboat, named Huki Pau, 
provided by our subcontractor, Sea Engineering, Inc.  The vessel has an overall length of 74 feet, 
a beam of 21 feet, and a light draft of 4 feet 3 inches, and is stabilized with a 4-point anchor 
system.  Our truck-mounted drill rig was driven onto the workboat, from which all the drilling 
was conducted.  The vessel has a wet well near the bow from which the drill string was extended 
to the mudline.  
The size of the borehole is on the order of about 6 inches in diameter, including an outer 
casing. The boreholes extended to depths of about 149.5 to 155.5 feet below the existing mudline, 
which is about 31 to 51 feet below the water surface.  Borings typically were advanced with PQ-size 
wireline coring methods to advance the PQ-size casing. Sampling of the soils was performed using 
the modified California split-barrel sampler (ASTM D3550), and the split-spoon sampler 
(ASTM D1586). In the softer soils, “relatively undisturbed” samples were obtained for testing by 
driving a ring-lined modified California split-barrel sampler (ASTM D3550) and pushing a 3-inch 
Outside Diameter (OD) thin-walled steel Shelby tube (ASTM D1587) into the soil formation. 
Where the borehole was advanced utilizing the PQ core barrel in the soil formations, 
samples of the soils recovered from the PQ core barrel were retained in core boxes for visual 
SECTION 4. GEOTECHNICAL EXPLORATION PROGRAM  
W.O. 8058-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 11 
Hawaii  California 
observation. In some cases, the borehole was advanced utilizing the PQ casing only as a rotary wash 
drilling method; therefore, there were no soil samples retrieved between the soil sampling points 
(soil sampling by the modified California split-barrel sampler or the split-spoon sampler for the 
Standard Penetration Test [SPT] was performed at maximum 10-foot intervals). The borings were 
advanced into the rock formations with PQ wireline coring methods using a double tube PQ rock 
core barrel. The PQ wireline coring methods produced rock core samples that are 3.35 inches in 
diameter. 
In-situ testing of the subsurface materials in the borings included the SPT, which was 
performed in accordance with ASTM D1586. Standard Penetration Test (SPT) sampling provides 
a measure of the resistance or consistency of the soil and is reported as the uncorrected N-value, 
which is defined as the number of blows required to drive a standard split-spoon sampler 
12 inches with a 140-lb. hammer, after 6 inches of initial seating. The penetration resistance of 
the Modified California Sampler was recorded in a similar manner, and the Modified California 
Sampler blows per 12 inches can be approximately related to the SPT N-value through 
correlation. Blow count correlation between the Modified California Sampler and the Standard 
Penetration Test Sampler is presented in Appendix E. 
Rock core recovery (REC) and rock quality designation (RQD) were measured as described 
in Appendix A. ASTM D6032 - Standard Test Method for Determining Rock Quality 
Designation (RQD) of Rock Core states that “Core sizes from BQ to PQ with core diameters of 36.5 
mm (1.44 in.) to 85 mm (3.35 in.), respectively, are normally acceptable for measuring RQD as 
long as proper drilling techniques are used that do not cause excessive core breakage or poor 
recovery, or both. The NX-size (54.7 mm [2.16 in.]) and NQ-size (47.5 mm [1.87 in.]) are the 
optimal core sizes for measuring RQD. The RQD is also useful for large core diameters provided 
the core diameter is clearly stated.” The rock core photographs are included in Appendix C for 
information and reference. 
4.2 
Geotechnical Laboratory Testing 
Laboratory testing was performed on soil and rock samples to determine the physical and 
engineering properties. Laboratory tests performed included moisture content, unit weight,

Atterberg Limits, grain size distribution, specific gravity, and corrosion potential tests (pH, 
minimum resistivity, chloride content, and sulfate content). The laboratory test results are 
provided in Appendix B. 
4.3 
Environmental Laboratory Testing 
Environmental laboratory testing was performed on the Investigation Derived 
Wastes (IDW) by our subcontractor, Element Environmental, LLC.  A total of six 55-gallon drums 
was generated for the IDW from the water boring work.  Results of the environmental screening 
and laboratory testing are provided in Appendix F for ease of reference.

SECTION 5.  
LIMITATIONS 
The geotechnical field and laboratory test data submitted in this geotechnical data report 
are based upon information obtained from the field exploration points. The coordinates of the 
proposed boring locations were surveyed by ControlPoint Surveying, Inc. and the as-drilled 
locations were obtained using a Juniper Systems Geode GNSS device. 
The stratification lines shown on the graphic representations of the field exploration 
points depict the approximate boundaries between soil and rock types and, as such, may denote 
a gradual transition.

client, and the U.S. Department of the Navy for specific application to the PHNSY Dry Dock 3 
Replacement Project, Pearl Harbor Naval Shipyard & IMF, JBPHH, Hawaii in accordance with 
generally accepted geotechnical engineering principles and practices. No warranty is expressed 
or implied.  
This geotechnical data report was not intended to investigate the potential presence of 
hazardous materials existing at the project site. It should be noted that the equipment, 
techniques, and personnel used to conduct a geo-environmental exploration differ substantially 
from those applied in geotechnical engineering.

APPENDIX A  
W.O. 8058-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
     Page A-1 
Hawaii  California 
A P P E N D I X   A 
Test Boring Exploration 
We explored the subsurface conditions at selected locations across the project site by 
drilling and sampling six in-water borings, designated as Boring Nos. WB-1, WB-2, WB-3, WB-4, 
WB-5, and WB-6A. The borings extended to depths ranging from about 185 to 200.5 feet below 
the water surface using a truck-mounted drill rig equipped with rotary coring tools. The water 
depth was measured at the start of  drilling with the water level taken as Mean Sea Level (MSL) 
to determine mudline elevation. No adjustments for tide were made. The mean tide range at 
Pearl Harbor (Bishop Point) is 1.3 feet. The field exploration was conducted from July 13 through 
August 8, 2020.  The approximate boring locations are shown on the Boring Location Plan, Plate 2. 
The overwater work was conducted from a twin-screw workboat, named Huki Pau, 
provided by our subcontractor, Sea Engineering, Inc.  The vessel has an overall length of 74 feet, 
a beam of 21 feet, and a light draft of 4 feet 3 inches, and is stabilized with a 4-point anchor 
system.  Our truck-mounted drill rig was driven onto the workboat, from which all the drilling 
was conducted.  The vessel has a wet well near the bow from which the drill string extended to 
the mudline. The size of the water borehole was on the order of about 6 inches in diameter, 
including an outer casing.  Borings typically were advanced with PQ-size wireline coring methods 
to advance the PQ-size casing. 
Our engineer/geologist classified the materials encountered in the borings by visual and 
textural examination in the field in general accordance with ASTM D2488, Standard Practice for 
Description and Identification of Soils, and monitored the drilling operations on a 
near-continuous basis. These classifications were further reviewed visually and by testing in the 
laboratory. Soils were classified in general accordance with ASTM D2487, Standard Practice for 
Classification of Soils for Engineering Purposes (Unified Soil Classification System), as shown on 
the Soil Log Legend, Plate A-0.1. Deviations made to the soil classification in accordance with 
ASTM D2487 are described on the Soil Classification Log Key, Plate A-0.2. Graphic representations 
of the materials encountered and other observations, such as circulation water condition, drilling 
rate, etc. are presented on the Logs of Borings, Plates A-1.1 through A-6.6. 
Relatively “undisturbed” soil samples were obtained from the borings drilled in general 
accordance with ASTM D3550, Standard Practice for Thick Wall, Ring-Lined, Spilt Barrel, Drive 
Sampling of Soils, by driving a 3-inch OD modified California sampler with a 140-pound hammer 
falling 30 inches. In addition, some samples were obtained from the borings drilled in general 
accordance with ASTM D1586, Standard Test Method for Penetration Test and Split-Barrel 
Sampling of Soils, by driving a 2-inch OD standard penetration sampler using the same hammer 
and drop. The blow counts needed to drive the sampler the second and third 6 inches of an 
18-inch drive are shown as the “Penetration Resistance” on the Logs of Borings at the appropriate 
sample depths.  Relatively “undisturbed” samples of the soft soil formation were obtained by 
pushing a 3-inch OD thin-walled stainless steel Shelby tube into the soil formation in general 
accordance with ASTM D1587, Standard Practice for Thin-Walled Tube Sampling of Soils for 
Appendix A 
Test Boring Exploration 
W.O. 8058-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
     Page A-2 
Hawaii • California 
Geotechnical Purposes. The range of hydraulic pressures needed to advance the sampler through 
a 24-inch push is shown as the “Penetration Resistance” on the Logs of Borings at the appropriate 
sample depths. 
Pocket penetrometer tests were performed on selected cohesive soil samples retrieved 
in the field. The pocket penetrometer test provides an indication of the unconfined compressive 
strength of the sample. Pocket penetrometer test results are summarized on the Logs of Borings 
at the appropriate sample depths. 
Core samples of the rock materials encountered at the project site were obtained by using 
diamond core drilling techniques, consisting of a PQ wireline coring system with double tube core 
barrel (core size of 3.35 inches), in general accordance with ASTM D2113, Diamond Core Drilling 
for Site Investigation. Core drilling is a rotary drilling method that uses a hollow bit to cut into the 
rock formation. The rock material left in the hollow core of the bit is mechanically recovered for 
examination and description. The coring rates indicated on the Logs of Borings refer to the time 
required to complete that particular core run at that depth. Rock cores were described in general 
accordance with the Rock Description System, as shown on the Rock Log Legend, Plate A-0.3. The 
Rock Description System is based generally on the publication “Suggested Methods for the 
Quantitative Description of Discontinuities in Rock Masses” by the International Society for Rock 
Mechanics (March 1977). 
Recovery (REC) is defined as the actual length of material recovered from a coring attempt 
versus the length of the core attempt. For example, if 3.7 feet of material is recovered from a 
5.0-foot core run, the recovery would be 74 percent and would be shown on the Logs of Borings 
as REC = 74%. 
The Rock Quality Designation (RQD) is a guide to the relative quality of rock masses. RQD 
is defined as the percentage of the total core run in rock that is sound material in excess of 4 
inches in length without any discontinuities, discounting any drilling, mechanical, and handling 
induced fractures or breaks. If 2.5 feet of sound material is recovered from a 5.0-foot core run in 
rock, the RQD would be 50 percent and would be shown on the Logs of Borings as RQD = 50%. 
Generally, the following is used to describe the relative quality of the rock based on the "Practical 
Handbook of Physical Properties of Rocks and Minerals” by Robert S. Carmichael (1989).

PRIMARY constituents are composed of the largest
percent of the soil mass. Primary constituents are
capitalized and bold (i.e., GRAVEL, SAND)
PRIMARY constituents are based on plasticity. Primary
constituents are capitalized and bold (i.e., CLAY, SILT)
SECONDARY constituents are composed of a
percentage less than the primary constituent. If the soil
mass consists of 12 percent or more fines content, a
cohesive constituent is used (SILTY or CLAYEY);
otherwise, a granular constituent is used (GRAVELLY
or SANDY) provided that the secondary constituent
consists of 20 percent or more of the soil mass.
Secondary constituents are capitalized and bold (i.e.,
SANDY GRAVEL, CLAYEY SAND) and precede the
primary constituent.
SECONDARY constituents are composed of a
percentage less than the primary constituent, but more
than 20 percent of the soil mass. Secondary constituents
are capitalized and bold (i.e., SANDY CLAY, SILTY
CLAY, CLAYEY SILT) and precede the primary
constituent.

Rock Log Legend
The following terms describe the resistance of a rock to indentation or scratching:
Specimen breaks with difficulty after several "pinging" hammer blows.
Example: Dense, fine grain volcanic rock
Specimen breaks with some difficulty after several hammer blows.
Example: Vesicular, vugular, coarse-grained rock
Specimen can be broked by one hammer blow. Cannot be scraped by knife. SPT may penetrate by
~25 blows per inch with bounce.
Example: Porous rock such as clinker, cinder, and coral reef
Can be indented by one hammer blow. Can be scraped or peeled by knife. SPT can penetrate by
~100 blows per foot.
Example: Weathered rock, chalk-like coral reef
Crumbles under hammer blow. Can be peeled and carved by knife. Can be indented by finger
pressure.
Example: Saprolite

APPENDIX B

A P P E N D I X   B 
Laboratory Tests 
Samples recovered from our field exploration program performed for the project were 
visually examined and classified in our laboratory in Honolulu, Hawaii. Selected samples were 
tested to evaluate the physical characteristics and engineering properties of the soils 
encountered. The laboratory testing program was assigned by Bechtel National, Inc. 
The laboratory tests performed included determination of natural moisture content, dry 
density, Atterberg Limits, sieve analysis and hydrometer, specific gravity, and corrosion tests. 
Results of the tests performed are presented on Plates B-1 through B-17. Detailed descriptions 
of the various tests performed for this exploration are presented below. 
Moisture and Density 
Moisture Content and Unit Weight tests were performed on selected soil samples to aid 
in defining the interface between the various soil layers encountered and correlating the layers 
between test borings. The moisture content and unit weight tests were performed in general 
accordance with ASTM D2216 and ASTM D7263, respectively. The test results are presented on 
the Logs of Borings at the appropriate sample depths. 
Atterberg Limits 
Forty-eight Atterberg Limits tests were performed on selected soil samples to evaluate 
the liquid limit (LL), plastic limit (PL), and plasticity index (PI), as an aid in classifying the soils. The 
Atterberg Limits tests were performed in general accordance with ASTM D4318 (Method A). The 
test results are summarized on the Logs of Borings at the appropriate sample depths. Graphic 
presentations of the test results are provided on Plates B-1 through B-5. 
Sieve Analysis 
To provide information on the particle size distribution of the soils encountered, 
thirty-four Sieve Analysis tests were performed to evaluate the gradation characteristics and to 
aid in soil classification. The tests were performed in accordance with ASTM D6913. Graphic 
presentations of the test results are provided on Plates B-6 through B-12. In addition, twenty-six 
tests were conducted on selected soil samples to evaluate the amount of fines content. The tests 
were performed in accordance with ASTM D1140. The amount of fines content from all the 
gradation tests are summarized on Plates B-13 through B-15. 
Specific Gravity 
Four Specific Gravity tests were performed on selected soil samples in general accordance 
with ASTM D854. The test results are presented on Plate B-16. 
Appendix C 
Laboratory Tests 
W.O. 8058-10

     Page B-2 
Hawaii  California 
Chemical Analyses and Soil Resistivity 
Seven pH (ASTM G51), six minimum resistivity (ASTM G57), and seven chloride (EPA 300) 
and sulfate (EPA 300) content tests were performed by our office or Eurofins TestAmerica on 
selected soil samples obtained from our field exploration. The test results are presented on 
Plate B-17.

APPENDIX D  
W.O. 8058-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
     Page D-1 
Hawaii • California 
A P P E N D I X   D 
Hammer Efficiency Calibration 
Standard penetration test energy measurement calibration was performed for the drill 
rig and sampling hammer used on the project. The drill rig used for this project consisted of 
CME-75DR. The drill rig was equipped with automatic-trip safety hammer. The energy delivered 
to the sampling rod for the drill rig and hammer combination was measured to determine the 
appropriate energy correction factor for the drill rig and hammer system. The dynamic testing 
was performed by Geolabs personnel in general accordance with ASTM D4633, “Standard Test 
Method for Energy Measurement for Dynamic Penetrometers.”  
Instrumentation 
For all the recorded tests, the instrumentation consisted of a 24-inch long AWJ rod 
subsection (1.75 OD/1.25 ID). The rod was equipped with two full-bridge foil resistance strain 
gages. Two piezo-resistive accelerometer transducers also were attached to the instrumented 
subsection. An SPT Analyzer Model SPT-8, manufactured by Pile Dynamics, Inc., was used to 
collect the data. The calibration and compliance certificates for the SPT Analyzer are presented 
in Figures D-1 and D-2 for information purposes only.  The SPT Analyzer is a computer fitted with 
a data acquisition and signal conditioning system. During driving of the drill string, the strain and 
acceleration signals are recorded and processed for each hammer blow. The strain signal is 
converted to a force record, and the acceleration signal is converted to a velocity record. The SPT 
Analyzer saves selected hammer blows containing this information to disk and determines the 
compressive stresses, displacement, and energy at the point of measurement. This information 
can be viewed on the computer screen during driving. 
Energy Measurement 
The primary purpose of the testing was to determine the energy transferred from the 
hammer to the instrumented subsection. The SPT Analyzer measurements of force and velocity 
were analyzed to calculate the energy using the FV method (EFV), which is based on both force 
and velocity measurements as follows:  
EFV = ∫ F(t) v(t) dt 
Another method, known as the EF2 method, was not used due to the limitations 
associated with the measurements (i.e., based on force alone assuming proportionality at the 
gauge locations because velocity is not used in the analysis). The EF2 method is not the 
recommended method in ASTM D4633. 
Appendix D 
Hammer Efficiency Calibration 
W.O. 8058-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
     Page D-2 
Hawaii • California 
The rated energy (designated as ER) for the hammer is 0.35 kip-feet based on the 2.5 feet 
(30 inch) drop height and 0.14 kip (140 pound) ram. The actual efficiency or energy transfer ratio 
(ETR) for the hammer system can then be determined using EFV and ER as follows:  
ETR = (EFV/ER)*100% 
For SPT, the typical ETR is taken as 60%, based on history and experience from prior 
studies. To adjust the measured SPT N values (Nmeasured) for actual hammer performance, the ETR 
can be used to correct the Nmeasured to N60 by using the correction factor (CN) determined as 
follows: 
CN = ETR/60% 
and N60 = CN * Nmeasured 
This is a correction for energy only. The application of additional corrections for 
overburden, non-conventional samplers, etc., was not considered in this study and should be 
evaluated for appropriateness by the geotechnical engineer for this project. 
Results 
The results of the testing program are presented in Plates D-1.1 through D-1.8 for drill rig 
CME-75DR. The results include FMX (maximum measured force in the rod subsection), VMX 
(maximum measured velocity in the rod subsection), BPM (blows per minute), EFV, and ETR. The 
blow count, including the SPT N-value, and the soil description were recorded by Geolabs 
personnel. 
The data was presented with statistical information, including the average, maximum, 
minimum and standard deviation for the blows associated with the group of data sets (sample 
depths) for the hammer. In addition, the data for all depths for a particular hammer combined 
are also presented in the tables.  
Note that the total blows may not match the number of blows analyzed due to differences 
between the blow count observations and the PDA recorded number. In some cases, bad blows 
or unreliable data from the PDA may have been omitted.  
The following table summarizes the hammer efficiency calibration results of the tests 
conducted for the drill rig CME-75DR used for the water boring exploration work.

APPENDIX E

A P P E N D I X   E 
Modified California Sampler and Standard Penetration Test Sampler Blow Count Correlation 
Blow count correlation between the Standard Penetration Test (SPT) split-spoon sampler 
and the Modified California split barrel sampler was developed with side-by-side boreholes using 
both samplers at the same depths.  The following table summarizes the samplers used in the 
side-by-side correlation. 
SAMPLERS 
Sampler Type 
Outer Diameter 
(inches) 
Inner Diameter 
(inches) 
Standard Penetration Test 
(SPT) Sampler  
2.0 
1.375 
Modified California Sampler 
(MCS) 
3.0 
2.42 
(fitted with internal rings or 
brass liners) 
The SPT results typically are reported as the N-value, which is defined as the number of 
blows required to drive a standard split-spoon sampler 12 inches with a 140-pound hammer after 
6 inches of initial seating. The penetration resistance of the larger diameter modified California 
sampler is also recorded as blows per 12 inches and can be approximately related to the 
SPT N-value through correlation to determine an equivalent SPT N-value. 
The SPT N-values used for this comparison were corrected based on the efficiency of the 
hammer-rod energy transfer for the various drill rigs used to drive each sampler and are 
presented as N60 values on the SPT-MCS Blow Count Correlation Table, Table 1. A total of 
40 driven samplers from our other projects were used to develop the side-by-side correlation. 
The samplers were driven in various deposits consisting of coral detritus, recent and older 
alluvium, estuarine and lagoonal deposits, and beach deposits. The data used for the side-by-side 
correlation is presented on the Modified California Sampler Versus Standard Penetration Test 
Sampler Correlation Table, Table E-1. 
Correlation between the SPT and MCS drive samplers was developed by comparing 
N60 values obtained from each sampler at the same depth in side-by-side boreholes. The 
side-by-side boreholes were located a maximum distance of about 15 feet apart. In addition, 
N60 values from the SPT and MCS samplers were compared within the same borehole at depths 
no greater than 5 feet and in the same material. Each SPT-MCS comparison was carefully selected 
to ensure uniformity of soil consistency and stratigraphy between each sampling event. 
Based on the results of the side-by-side comparison, a regression analysis of the 
presented data suggest that the equivalent SPT N-values would be about 56 percent of those 
recorded with the Modified California Sampler. A data plot with the regression analysis plotting 
Appendix E

each comparison is presented on the Modified California Sampler Versus Standard Penetration 
Test Sampler Correlation Plot, Plate E-1.

HDOH Environmental Action Levels (EALs) for sites where groundwater IS NOT a current or potential source of drinking water and the site is less than 150 meters to a surface water body (HDOH Fall 2017). 
ND = The sample is not detected above the laboratory detection limit
ND = The sample specific detection limit does not support the regulatory requirement
mg/kg = milligrams per kilogram
D = The reported value is from a dilution.
J = Estimated: The analyte was positively identified; the quantitation is an estimation
J1 = Estimated: The quantitation is an estimation due to discrepancies in meeting certain analyte-specific quality control criteria
M = Manual integrated compound
q = Estimated maximum possible concentration, quantitated using the theoretical ion ratio. The measured ion ratio does not meet qualitative ID criteria and indicates a possible interference.
NS = No Standard
Analytes with NS and ND are not presented. 
Analytes ND with DL less than the HDOH EAL are not presented.

Narrative
Job Narrative
320-63852-1
Receipt 
The samples were received on 8/20/2020 9:35 AM; the samples arrived in good condition, and where required, properly preserved and on 
ice.  The temperature of the cooler at receipt was 3.4º C.
GC/MS VOA 
Method 8260C DOD: The continuing calibration verification (CCV) associated with batch 320-408064 recovered above the upper control 
limit for Bromomethane and Trichlorofluoromethane.  The samples associated with this CCV were non-detects for the affected analytes; 
therefore, the data have been reported.  The associated samples are impacted: 200035-D1-WB6A-S (320-63852-1), 200035-D2-WB5-S 
(320-63852-2), 200035-D3-WB4-S (320-63852-3), 200035-D4-WB3-S (320-63852-4), 200035-D5-WB2-S (320-63852-5), 
200035-D6-WB1-S (320-63852-6) and (CCV 320-408064/3). 
Method 8260C DOD: The laboratory control sample (LCS) and / or laboratory control sample duplicate (LCSD) for preparation batch 
320-406481 and analytical batch 320-409287 recovered outside control limits for the following analytes: 1,2,3-Trichlorobenzene.  These 
analytes were biased high in the LCS and were not detected in the associated samples; therefore, the data have been reported.
Method 8260C DOD: The continuing calibration verification (CCV) associated with batch 320-409287 recovered above the upper control 
limit for 1,2,3-Trichlorobenzene.  The samples associated with this CCV were non-detects for the affected analytes; therefore, the data 
have been reported.  The associated sample is impacted: (CCV 320-409287/2). 
Method 8260C DOD: The RPD of the laboratory control sample (LCS) and laboratory control sample duplicate (LCSD) for preparation 
batch 320-406481 and analytical batch 320-409287 recovered outside control limits for the following analytes: 2-Butanone (MEK) and 
Acetone.  
No additional analytical or quality issues were noted, other than those described above or in the Definitions/Glossary page.
GC/MS Semi VOA 
Methods 8270C, 8270D: The following samples were diluted due to  the nature of the sample matrix:  (320-63852-A-4-AC MS) and 
(320-63852-A-4-AD MSD). Because of this dilution, the surrogate spike and matrix spike concentration in the sample was reduced to a 
level where the recovery calculation does not provide useful information.
Methods 8270C, 8270D: The matrix spike / matrix spike duplicate (MS/MSD) recoveries for preparation batch 320-405646 and 320-408076 
and analytical batch 320-408587 were outside control limits.  Sample matrix interference and/or non-homogeneity are suspected because 
the associated laboratory control sample (LCS) recovery was within acceptance limits.
Methods 8270C, 8270D: The following samples were diluted due to the nature of the sample matrix: 200035-D3-WB4-S (320-63852-3), 
200035-D4-WB3-S (320-63852-4), 200035-D5-WB2-S (320-63852-5), 200035-D6-WB1-S (320-63852-6), (320-63852-A-4-AE DU), 
(320-63852-A-4-AC MS), (320-63852-A-4-AD MSD) and (320-63852-A-4-AF TRL).  Elevated reporting limits (RLs) are provided.
Methods 8270C SIM, 8270D SIM: The matrix spike / matrix spike duplicate (MS/MSD) recoveries and precision for preparation batch 
320-405646 and 320-408031 and analytical batch 320-408409 was outside control limits.   Sample matrix interference and 
non-homogeneity is suspected.
Methods 8270C SIM, 8270D SIM: The response for the internal standard Perylene-d12 was outside of acceptance limits for the following 
sample: (320-63852-A-1-Y MSD).  The sample shows evidence of matrix interference.
Method 8270D SIM: The following sample was diluted due to the nature of the sample matrix: 200035-D3-WB4-S (320-63852-3).  Elevated 
reporting limits (RLs) are provided.
No additional analytical or quality issues were noted, other than those described above or in the Definitions/Glossary page.

limits. The associated samples were re-analyzed following a successful CCV resulting in repeated failure of the closing CCV , indicating 
that the sample matrix is adversely affecting the instrument and causing the failures.

200035-D5-WB2-S (320-63852-5) and (320-63852-A-4-AM DU).  Evidence of matrix interference is present; therefore, re-extraction and 
re-analysis was not performed.
Method 8081B: The matrix spike / matrix spike duplicate (MS/MSD) recoveries and precision for preparation batch 320-407149 and 
320-408439 and analytical batch 320-411024 were outside control limits.  Sample matrix interference and/or non-homogeneity are 
suspected because the associated laboratory control sample (LCS) recovery was within acceptance limits.

the following samples: 200035-D1-WB6A-S (320-63852-1), 200035-D3-WB4-S (320-63852-3) and 200035-D6-WB1-S (320-63852-6).  
The lower value(s) has been reported and qualified in accordance with the laboratory's SOP.  8151  preparation batch 280-507696 and 
analytical batch 280-508511

criteria. The reanalysis of CPS was performed after the CCV and prior to all the samples. The CPS being analyzed after the CCV has no 
adverse impact on the data. 200035-D1-WB6A-S (320-63852-1), 200035-D2-WB5-S (320-63852-2), 200035-D3-WB4-S (320-63852-3),

were not taken prior to grinding, sieving and weighing the sample aliquots. However, samples were set to dry 3 days prior to grinding, 
sieving and weighing. Methods affected are 8015B_DRO, 8081A, 8082, 8151., and 6020.

Method 3546: The following samples required a mercury clean-up, via EPA Method 3660A, to reduce matrix interferences caused by sulfur:

number used was: M27G026.
Method 8151A: Due to the matrix, the initial volume(s) used for the following samples deviated from the standard procedure: 
200035-D1-WB6A-S (320-63852-1), 200035-D2-WB5-S (320-63852-2), 200035-D3-WB4-S (320-63852-3), 200035-D4-WB3-S

APPENDIX G 
July 1, 2020 
Memorandum 
To: Andrew Rocheleau, Sea Engineering, Inc. 
From: Steve Dollar and Andrea Millan, MRCI 
Subject: Geolabs Borehole Site Inspection 
On June 28, 2020, we inspected the six proposed Geolab borehole locations (WB1 – 
WB6) and three offshore Huki Pau anchor point locations (HP1 – HP3). The purpose of 
our inspection was to identify any biotic resources at the selected locations. Prior to our 
work borehole sites were located by a surveyor; anchor sites were located with a 
handheld GPS. Each identified location was marked with a lead weight and surface 
buoy. Our inspection protocol consisted of visual examination and photographic 
documentation of bottom composition within a radius of approximately 15 feet (ft) from 
the marker weight. 
Results of our inspection revealed that locations WB2 – WB5, HP1, and HP2 occurred on 
homogeneous soft sediment bottom with no surrounding biota. The original locations for 
WB6 and HP3 occurred on semi-solid sloping surfaces that were inhabited by small coral 
colonies. At these locations, the marker weights were moved 10 – 15 ft to areas of 
uniform soft sediment. The original location for WB1 included a large boulder that would 
not have been amenable to drilling. For this reason, the marker weight was relocated 
approximately 5 ft to an area of uniform soft sediment. 
Listed below are the depth and bottom composition type for each of the nine 
locations. 
Representative photographs from each of the final locations are attached. 
Thank you for the opportunity to work with you on this project. Please let us know if you 
have any questions.
